Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the second argument successfully proves that possibility cannot be existent because it seems to show a regress.
Correction: The commentator labels it as 'takalluf' (forced) because it requires additional premises not present in the first argument. Moreover, the regress is avoided if possibility is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, not a separate effect.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the second argument 'takalluf' (forced exertion)?
A) Because it uses an infinite regress.
B) Because it requires additional premises not needed in the first argument.
C) Because it assumes possibility is a relation.
D) Because it contradicts the first argument.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against possibility is weak because it relies on extra assumptions, making it a forced effort rather than a rigorous proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator describes the second argument as 'takalluf'—a forced effort with extra assumptions. Are there teachings or practices in your faith that you follow merely out of habit or pressure, without being convinced of their rational foundation? Will you seek clarity even if it means letting go of comfortable but unexamined beliefs?
?
This passage distinguishes two meanings of 'necessity': one about cause-effect priority and another about a thing's intrinsic mode. When you say 'Allah is necessary,' do you truly grasp that His necessity is essential, not derived? How might confusing these affect the quality of your worship and reliance on Him?
?
The argument shows that possibility is prior to existence—you were possible before you existed. How does that reality humble you? Commit to one concrete way you will thank your Creator today for bringing you from mere possibility into being.

The Second Argument for Imkan and Its Application to Wujub
Al-Jurjani explains the second argument that imkan is prior to existence and applies it to wujub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that imkan (contingency) is a mental consideration, not a positive attribute, and how it applies to wujub (necessity).

Core Idea
Imkan and wujub are mental constructs, not real attributes.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a sophisticated argument demonstrating that both imkan and wujub are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya), not positive attributes inhering in things. Al-Jurjani first presents a forced application of an earlier argument to imkan, then offers a more direct 'second face' that shows imkan's priority over existence. Finally, he applies this same reasoning to wujub, citing Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style of kalam reasoning.
2
Understand the forced argument
Follow the hypothetical: if imkan were an attribute, it would be either essence/part (invalid) or superadded (making it an effect, leading to regress).
This shows imkan cannot be a positive attribute.
3
Grasp the second face and application
Learn the direct argument: imkan is prior to existence, so it cannot be a positive attribute (which are posterior). See how this applies to wujub as well.
This second face is more straightforward.

قوله : ( وقد يتكلف وجه التكلف احتياجه في إبطال الزيادة إلى مقدمات غير مذكورة فيما سيأتي في إبطال زيادة الوجوب فلا يلائمه الحوالة المذكورة بقوله : وسنبطله . قوله : ( وإلا لم تكن ممكنة في حد ذاتها لا يخفى أن هذا إنما يقتضي أن تكون الماهية في نفسها مقتضية له بحيث لا يتصور انفكاكه عنها، فأينما وجدت كانت متصفة به كما هو حكم لوازم الماهية وهذا لا ينافي كونه معلولاً لغيرها؛ لجواز أن تكون الماهية مع لوازمها معلولة له بحيث لا يتصور الانفكاك بينهما أصلاً كما قالوا إن جعل الماهية جعل للوازمها . نعم لا يمكن استفادتها الإمكان من غيرها بأن يكون متأخراً عنها حاصلاً بعدها، فإنه يستلزم الانقلاب. قوله: (يمكن وجوده في نفسه إلخ) فالإمكان مقدم على الوجود بالذات بمراتب؛ لتقدمه على الاحتياج المتقدم على الإيجاد المتقدم على الوجود، وقد يتقدم زماناً أيضاً كما في الممكنات الحادثة، ولظهور التقدم في الإمكان قال : وربما يستعمل في الوجوب . قوله : ( وقد يتكلف إلخ وجه التكلف أنه يحتاج في إجراء ذلك إلى مقدمات زائدة ليست بصريحة في الوجه الثاني ولا هي مما يمكن اعتبارها بالقياس إلى الوجوب، وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( والعلة متقدمة على المعلول (بالوجوب) أي بوجوب الوجود، لأن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يجب وما لم يجب لم يوجد فبطل ما يتوهم من أن هذا الوجوب كيفية نسبة التقدم إلى العلة لا كيفية نسبة الوجوب إلى الممكن والوجوب الذاتي المستحيل في الممكن إنما هو الكيفية الثانية لا الأولى .
عنه ) أي عن الوجود فإن قيام الصفة الموجودة بموصوفها فرع لوجوده، فلا يكون الإمكان صفة موجودة. وربما يستعمل هذا الوجه الآخر (في الوجوب) كما استعمله الإمام الرازي فيقال : الوجوب سابق على الوجود سبقاً ذاتياً (لأن إيجاب ماهيته لوجوده يستتبع وجوده عقلا ولذلك صح أن يقال : اقتضى ذاته وجوده فوجود الصفة الثبوتية يستحيل أن يسبق على وجود موصوفها سبقاً ذاتياً.
And one may labor to apply the second [argument] to imkan (contingency), saying: if it were existent, it would be either the essence of the possible or a part of it, but both are invalid because it is a relation between essence and existence, or it would be superadded to it and inhering in it, thus being an effect of it, since it is impossible for it to derive its essential imkan from another, otherwise it would not be possible in itself, and the cause is prior to the effect by wujub (necessity); that wujub is either essential, which is impossible for the possible, or by another, and wujub by another is a branch of essential imkan, so the possible would have another imkan before its imkan. And another face: that imkan is prior to existence because a thing can exist in itself and then comes to existence from another, whereas a positive attribute is posterior. His statement: (And one may labor [explanation]): the reason for the labor is its need for premises not mentioned in what will come concerning invalidating the superaddition of wujub, so the reference mentioned by his statement 'and we will invalidate it' does not suit it. His statement: (otherwise it would not be possible in itself): it is not hidden that this only requires that the essence in itself necessitates it such that separation from it is inconceivable, so wherever it exists it is characterized by it, as is the case with essential concomitants, and this does not negate its being an effect of another; for it is possible that the essence together with its concomitants is an effect of that other such that separation between them is absolutely inconceivable, as they said: making the essence is making its concomitants. Indeed, it cannot derive imkan from another such that it is posterior to it and occurs after it, for that entails change. His statement: (it can exist in itself etc.): for imkan is prior to existence by many essential degrees; because it is prior to need which is prior to bringing into existence which is prior to existence, and it may also be temporally prior as in temporal possibles, and due to the obviousness of priority in imkan he said: and perhaps it is used for wujub. His statement: (and one may labor etc.): the reason for the labor is that it requires additional premises which are not explicit in the second face and which cannot be considered in comparison to wujub, and it is obvious. His statement: (and the cause is prior to the effect by wujub): meaning by the necessity of existence, because a thing does not exist until it is necessary, and it is not necessary until it exists, so what is imagined that this wujub is a quality of the relation of priority to the cause and not the quality of the relation of wujub to the possible is invalidated; and the essential wujub impossible for the possible is only the second quality, not the first. (About it): i.e. about existence, for the subsistence of an existing attribute in its subject is a branch of its existence, so imkan is not an existing attribute. And perhaps this other face is used for wujub as Imam al-Razi used it, saying: wujub is prior to existence by essential priority, because the necessitation of its essence for its existence entails its existence intellectually, and therefore it is correct to say: its essence demanded its existence, so the existence of a positive attribute cannot be prior to the existence of its subject by essential priority.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (وجه آخر) in the argument that imkan (contingency) and wujub (necessity) are mental considerations (amoor i'tibariyya), not positive attributes. The author begins by explaining the forced application of the second argument to imkan. If imkan were a positive existent attribute, it would have to be either the essence of the possible or a part of it, but that is invalid because imkan is a relation between essence and existence. Alternatively, it would be an accident inhering in the possible, making the possible its cause; but it is impossible for a thing to derive its essential imkan from another, because then it would not be essentially possible. The cause must be prior by necessity, and that necessity is either essential (impossible for a possible) or by another, which itself depends on essential imkan, leading to an infinite regress. Then the second face is stated: imkan is prior to existence because a thing's possibility for existence is prior to its actual existence, whereas a positive attribute is posterior to the existence of its subject. The commentary clarifies the subtle points, such as the meaning of 'priority' and the objection that if imkan were a positive attribute, it would have to be prior to existence, which is impossible. The passage also applies this face to wujub, citing Imam al-Razi: wujub is prior to existence by essential priority, because the very nature of the necessary being entails existence, and a positive attribute cannot be prior to the existence of its subject. This demonstrates that both imkan and wujub are not positive attributes but mental considerations.

The Argument from Impossibility of Posteriority and the Two Principles
Al-Jurjani presents a sufficient proof based on impossibility of posteriority and states two underlying principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani proves that wujub and imkan are non-existential (mental considerations) using the impossibility of posteriority and how he derives two underlying principles.

Core Idea
If an attribute cannot be posterior to its subject, it is not a real attribute—it is a mental consideration.

This passage presents a clever argument that avoids the need to prove priority. By focusing on the impossibility of posteriority, al-Jurjani establishes a criterion for identifying mental attributes. He then encapsulates the logic in two principles from the author of al-Talwihat, which form the basis of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall the debate about whether wujub (necessity) and imkan (possibility) are real positive attributes or mere mental considerations. This argument aims to show they are mental.
The argument is part of a larger discussion on the ontological status of modal attributes.
2
Read the argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key premise: imkan and wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of their subject, unlike positive attributes which must be posterior.
This is the core move: impossibility of posteriority is sufficient.
3
Generalize the proof
Observe that the argument applies to any attribute whose posteriority is impossible, like origination (huduth). This shows the proof is not ad hoc.
This strengthens the case for the mental nature of such attributes.
4
Examine the two principles
Read the second part where a rule (dabit) containing two principles is given. The first principle states that any genus whose individuals are necessarily characterized by that genus is mental.
The first principle is the basis for the first face of the argument.
5
Connect principles to argument
See how the first principle applies: wujub and imkan are such that any subject must have them, so they are mental. The second principle relates to the sufficiency of impossibility of posteriority.
The two principles unify the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويكفينا ) في الاستدلال على كون الوجوب أو الإمكان أمراً عدمياً ( امتناع تأخره ) عن وجود الموصوف، فلا نحتاج في ذلك إلى بيان التقدم فلا يتوجه علينا أنا لا نسلم تقدمه لجواز أن يكون معه . وحينئذ نقول لا شبهة في أن الإمكان أو الوجوب يمتنع تأخره عن وجود موصوفه وكل صفة ثبوتية لا يمتنع تأخرها عن وجود موصوفها بل يجب تأخرها عنه ويكون هذا الدليل مطرداً في كل صفة يمتنع تأخرها عن وجود موصوفها كالحدوث ونظائره. (ضابط) يشتمل على قاعتين ذكرهما صاحب التلويحات إحداهما أساس الوجه الأول الدال على كون كل واحد من الوجوب والإمكان أمراً اعتبارياً. والثانية : أساس الوجه الآخر الذي استعمل في الوجوب أيضا، إذا اكتفي فيه بامتناع التأخر. إن كل ما تكرر نوعه أي يتصف أي شخص يفرض منه بمفهومه فهو اعتباري ) أي كل نوع كان بحيث إذا فرض أن فرداً منه أي فرد كان موجوداً وجب أن يتصف ذلك الفرد بذلك النوع حتى يوجد ذلك النوع
(And it suffices us) in inferring that wujub or imkan is a non-existential matter (the impossibility of its posteriority) to the existence of the described thing, so we do not need in that to show priority, and thus the objection that we do not grant its priority because it could be with it does not apply to us. Then we say: there is no doubt that imkan or wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of its subject, whereas every positive attribute is not impossible to be posterior to the existence of its subject, rather it must be posterior to it, and this proof is general to every attribute whose posteriority to the existence of its subject is impossible, such as origination and its like. (A rule) containing two principles mentioned by the author of al-Talwihat: one is the basis of the first face indicating that each of wujub and imkan is a mental consideration. And the second: the basis of the other face that was used for wujub as well, when one is satisfied with the impossibility of posteriority. Every genus whose type recurs, i.e., any person assumed from it is characterized by its concept, is mental. That is, every genus such that if an individual from it, any individual, is assumed to exist, that individual must be characterized by that genus in order for that genus to exist.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage provides a sufficient argument (يكفينا) for proving that wujub and imkan are non-existential (عدمية). The argument is based on the impossibility of their being posterior to the existence of their subject. Any positive attribute must be posterior to the existence of its subject because it depends on it. But imkan and wujub cannot be posterior; they must be either simultaneous or prior. Since they cannot be posterior, they are not positive attributes. This argument avoids the need to prove priority, which might be disputed. The author then gives a rule (ضابط) containing two principles: (1) the basis of the first face, which shows that each of wujub and imkan is mental; (2) the basis of the second face, which suffices with impossibility of posteriority. The first principle is: "Every genus whose instances, whenever any individual is assumed to exist, must be characterized by that genus, is mental." This means that if a concept is such that whenever an individual is supposed to exist, that individual must have that attribute in order for the genus to exist, then that attribute is a mental consideration.

الصفة الثبوتية
ضابط

الأمر الاعتباري

Misconception: That the impossibility of posteriority alone is not sufficient to prove mental consideration
Correction: The author shows it is sufficient and yields a general criterion that applies to wujub, imkan, and similar attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the argument from impossibility of posteriority?
A) Imkan and wujub must be prior to existence.
B) Imkan and wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of their subject.
C) All positive attributes are prior to existence.
D) Imkan and wujub are simultaneous with existence.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of posteriority serves as a decisive test: if an attribute cannot be posterior to its subject, it is not a real positive attribute but a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the impossibility of posteriority reveals that many of our attributes are mental considerations, how often do you mistake your own intellectual projections for the truth about Allah? Are you willing to let go of comfortable concepts so you can encounter Him as He is?

The First Principle: Every Recurring Genus is Mental
Al-Jurjani explains the first principle and lists four benefits from the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first principle of kalam: that every concept whose instances constitute a recurring genus is mental (i'tibārī), and the logical reasoning that supports it.

Core Idea
A concept that cannot be postponed from its instances is a mental concept.

Al-Jurjani unpacks the first principle of kalam: 'Every recurring genus is mental.' This principle is foundational for distinguishing between real attributes and mental considerations. In this passage, he explains the logical necessity of essential priority and lists four key benefits derived from the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Clarifying essential priority and impossibility of posteriority
The passage first clarifies that the priority meant is essential, not temporal, and then argues that posteriority is impossible for such concepts, otherwise change would be possible.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the logical foundation.
2
The logical argument and its generality
Al-Jurjani explains that the impossibility of posteriority fulfills the condition of the second figure in logic and yields a general proof without needing to establish priority separately.
This shows the elegance of the argument.
3
The principle defined and its scope
The principle states that whenever an individual of a genus is assumed to exist, it must be characterized by that genus, making the genus a mental concept. The commentator adds that the use of 'genus' is only for illustration.
Thus the principle applies to any concept with that property.
4
Four benefits and a caveat about non-existence
The commentator extracts four benefits from the interpretation, clarifying the meaning of 'recurrence', 'assumed', the word 'concept', and the pronoun. Finally, if existence is not assumed, the characteristic does not apply, as with imkan and wujub considered as non-existent.
This caveat prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( سبقا ذاتياً قيد هاهنا بالذات لامتناع السبق الزماني . قوله : ( يمتنع تأخره وإلا أمكن الانقلاب. قوله : ( بل يجب إلخ ) قال :أولاً : لا يمتنع ليتحقق شرط إنتاج الشكل الثاني أعني اختلاف المقدمتين بالإيجاب والسلب، ثم أضرب عنه لبيان أن ذلك السلب متحقق في ضمن الوجوب . قوله : ( ويكون إلخ) عطف على قوله : لا نحتاج إلخ يعني أن امتناع التأخر يسقط عنا مؤونة بيان التقدم ويفيد عموم الدليل . قوله : (أي كل نوع إلخ) لعل اعتبار النوع لمجرد التصوير وإلا فكل مفهوم يكون بتلك الحيثية يجب أن يكون اعتبارياً نوعاً كان أو غيره، وأشار الشارح قدس سره بهذا التفسير إلى فوائد :إحداها : أن المراد بتكرر النوع تكرره من حيث الوجود والثانية : أن المراد بقوله : يفرض منه فرضه موجوداً . والثالثة : أن لفظ المفهوم مقحم والمراد يتصف به. والرابعة : أن ضمير هو راجع إلى قوله : نوعه لا إلى ما كما يسبق إليه الوهم . قوله : ( إذا فرض إلخ) أما إذا لم يفرض وجوده فلا يجب اتصافه بذلك النوع كالإمكان والوجوب فإنهما إذا فرضا عدمين يكونان ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وانتفاء مبدأ المحمول لا يستلزم الحمل كما سيجيء.
His statement: (by essential priority) is qualified here by 'essential' because of the impossibility of temporal priority. His statement: (its posteriority is impossible) because otherwise change would be possible. His statement: (rather it must, etc.) He said: first, it is not impossible in order to fulfill the condition of producing the second figure, i.e., the difference of the two premises in affirmation and negation, then he turned away from that to show that that negation is realized within wujub. His statement: (and it is, etc.) is conjoined to his statement: 'we do not need', meaning that the impossibility of posteriority removes from us the burden of showing priority and yields the generality of the proof. His statement: (i.e., every genus, etc.) Perhaps the consideration of genus is only for illustration; otherwise every concept that has that property must be mental, whether genus or other. And the commentator, may God sanctify his secret, pointed out in this interpretation four benefits: first, that what is meant by 'recurrence of the type' is its recurrence from the standpoint of existence; second, that what is meant by his statement 'assumed from it' is assuming it as existent; third, that the word 'concept' is extra, and the meaning is 'characterized by it'; fourth, that the pronoun 'it' refers to his statement 'its type' and not to 'what' as might be imagined. His statement: (if assumed, etc.) As for if its existence is not assumed, then it is not necessary for it to be characterized by that genus, like imkan and wujub, for if they are assumed as non-existents, they are impossible of existence in the external world, and the non-existence of the subject of predication does not entail predication, as will come.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the first principle: "كل ما تكرر نوعه... فهو اعتباري". It first clarifies the meaning of 'essential priority' (سبقاً ذاتياً) and 'impossibility of posteriority'. Then it explains the logical structure of the argument, noting that the impossibility of posteriority eliminates the need to prove priority and yields a general proof. Then it elaborates on the definition of the principle: 'every genus whose type recurs' means that whenever any individual of that genus is assumed to exist, it must be characterized by that genus; such a concept is mental. The commentator points out that the term 'type' (نوع) is used only for illustration; any concept that has this property is mental. He then draws four benefits from the interpretation: (1) the recurrence of the type is from the standpoint of existence; (2) 'supposed from it' means supposing it as existent; (3) the word 'concept' (مفهوم) is extra, and the meaning is 'characterized by it'; (4) the pronoun 'it' (هو) refers to 'its type' (نوعه), not to something else as might be misunderstood. Finally, it notes that if existence is not assumed, the attribute is not necessarily predicated, as with imkan and wujub when assumed as non-existent.

Key Terms

مفهوم
اعتباري
حمل
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle only applies to genera.
Correction: The commentator explains that genus is only for illustration; any concept with the same property is mental.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the phrase 'every genus whose type recurs' mean?
A) The genus must have many instances.
B) Whenever an individual of that genus is assumed to exist, it must be characterized by that genus.
C) The genus must be a positive attribute.
D) The genus must be a mental concept.

Key Takeaway
The principle that every recurring genus is mental is established through the impossibility of posteriority and is applicable to any concept that cannot be postponed from its instances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider concepts like 'existence' or 'necessity' in your reflection on Allah's attributes, do you realize that these concepts are mental and not independent external realities? How might this affect the way you avoid likening Allah to created mental concepts? (Challenge: Commit tonight to reviewing your understanding of Allah's sifat with this principle in mind.)
?
Have you ever assumed that a concept must apply to something even when that thing does not exist? Think of a moment when you made a judgment about something absent or impossible. How does the caveat about assuming existence force you to reconsider that assumption? (Call to action: Before your next salah, examine one belief you hold about an unseen matter and see if you are inadvertently treating a mental concept as externally real.)
?
The commentator shows that the word 'concept' is extra and the real meaning is 'characterized by the genus'. Are there attributes or labels you use for yourself (like 'believer', 'sinner', 'wise') that you treat as fixed concepts rather than as characterizations of your actual state? How can you shift your self-understanding from static concepts to dynamic characterizations in light of this lesson? (Commit to asking someone you trust this week: 'Do I define myself by a concept or by my real character?')

The Refutation of Regress for Necessity
Al-Jurjani presents the author's refutation of the regress for necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that necessity of necessity prevents an infinite regress because something whose essence is necessity is necessary by itself.

Core Idea
Necessity is self-necessitating, so no regress can occur.

This lesson focuses on the author's specific objection to applying the regress argument to the attribute of necessity. The previous discussion raised the possibility of an infinite chain of necessities, but the author counters that necessity itself is a special case: its essence is identical to its existence as necessary. Therefore, no additional necessity is needed, and the regress is blocked. This passage clarifies that the regress only arises when there is another individual of the same type, which does not happen for necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms: وجوب الوجوب, المنع, التسلسل الباطل.
The passage is dense; it may help to read the translation alongside.
2
Understand the main claim
Identify the author's main claim: that the necessity of necessity itself prevents regress. Ask why that is so.
The key is the distinction between things whose essence is necessity and things whose essence is other.
3
Trace the example of descriptiveness
Follow the explanation about descriptiveness and implication as examples of considerational things where regress can be cut off by cutting off consideration.
This shows that regress only occurs if there is an actual individual, not a mere concept.
4
See why necessity is different
Contrast necessity with descriptiveness: necessity has no external individual; it is purely a relation of the intellect, so no regress follows.
The argument hinges on the nature of necessity as a self-subsisting attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : والمنع ما ذكرنا من أن وجوب الوجوب نفسه، وتلخيصه أن ما حقيقته غير الوجوب فإنه لا يكون واجباً إلا بوجوب يقوم به، وأما الذي حقيقته الوجوب فإنه واجب بذاته لا قوله فيكون هناك موصوفية أخرى هي صفة للموصوفية لاتصافها بأن الماهية موصوفة بها فلا يرد أن اللازم هاهنا موصوفية أخرى للماهية لا للموصوفية والمستفاد من القاعدة المذكورة أن يتصف الفرد بذلك النوع، والأظهر أن يقال : إن ذكر الاتصاف أيضاً بطريق التمثيل، فإن التسلسل المحال إنما يلزم من وجود فرد آخر من ذلك النوع، سواء كان قائماً بالفرد الأول أولا . قوله : ( لكان له تعين آخر لأن كل ما هو موجود في الخارج متعين. قوله : (ذلك التسلسل الباطل أي التسلسل في الأمور المترتبة الموجودة معاً بخلاف ما إذا لم تكن موجودة فإنه إما أن لا يوجد الآحاد أصلاً كما في الوجوب والإمكان والتعين، فإنها على تقدير كونها ممتنعة الوجود في الخارج لا يكون للوجوب ،وجوب، ولا للإمكان إمكان ، ولا للتعين تعين أو توجد الآحاد الاعتبارية وينقطع التسلسل بانقطاع الاعتبار كما في الموصوفية واللزوم، فإن العقل إذا لاحظ الموصوفية واللزوم من حيث إنه آلة لملاحظة الطرفين ورابطة بينهما لا يكون هناك موصوفية أخرى ولزوم آخر، وإذا لاحظهما قصداً أي من حيث إنهما مفهومان من المفهومات حكم بموصوفية الطرفين بهما وبلزوم اللزوم لهما، وحصل عند العقل موصوفية ثانية ولزوم ثان هما اللتان بملاحظة حال الموصوفية الأولى، واللزوم الأول بالقياس إلى الطرفين، ثم إذا لاحظهما قصداً وبالذات اعتبر موصوفية ثالثة ولزوم ثالث وهكذا الحال وإذا انقطع الاعتبار انقطع السلسلة.
The author said: The prevention is what we mentioned, that the necessity of necessity itself. Its summary is that what has an essence other than necessity is not necessary except by a necessity subsisting in it. But that whose essence is necessity is necessary by itself, not ... His saying: 'Then there would be another descriptiveness' is a description for descriptiveness because of its being described that the essence is described by it. So it is not objected that the necessary here is another descriptiveness for the essence, not for descriptiveness. And what is understood from the mentioned rule is that the individual is described by that type. The more apparent is to say that the mention of description is also by way of example. For the impossible regress only follows from the existence of another individual of that type, whether it subsists in the first individual or not. His saying: 'It would have another determination' because everything that exists in the outside is determined. His saying: 'That invalid regress' means the regress in ordered things that exist together, contrary to when they do not exist—for then either the individuals do not exist at all, as in necessity, contingency, and determination: for on the supposition that they are impossible of existence in the outside, necessity has no necessity, contingency has no contingency, and determination has no determination; or the considerational individuals exist and the regress is cut off by the cutting off of consideration, as in descriptiveness and implication. For if the intellect observes descriptiveness and implication as a tool for observing the two sides and a link between them, there is no other descriptiveness and another implication; and if it observes them intentionally—that is, as concepts among concepts—it judges that the two sides are described by them and that implication applies to them, and a second descriptiveness and a second implication obtain in the intellect, which are [obtained] by observing the state of the first descriptiveness and the first implication with respect to the two sides. Then if it observes them intentionally and essentially, it considers a third descriptiveness and a third implication, and so on. And when consideration is cut off, the chain is cut off.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the author's objection (المنع) to applying the regress argument to necessity. The author (al-Iji, as quoted by al-Jurjani) says that 'the necessity of necessity itself' prevents the regress. The summary is: anything whose essence is other than necessity only becomes necessary by a necessity that inheres in it. But something whose essence is necessity itself is necessary by itself, not through an acquired necessity. Thus, for necessity, there is no need for another necessity, so the regress stops. The commentary then explains that the previous example of 'another descriptiveness' was just illustrative, not a necessary outcome. The regress only occurs when there is another individual of the same type, but in the case of necessity, the type does not repeat because the essence is identical to the attribute. The passage also repeats the earlier explanation of the regress with considerational things, showing that the regress can be cut off by cutting off consideration. This reinforces that necessity is a special case: it cannot be considered as having an external individual, so the regress does not arise.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجوب
المنع
التسلسل الباطل
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the regress applies to necessity as well, requiring an infinite chain of necessities.
Correction: The author clarifies that the necessity of necessity itself is self-necessitating, so no regress occurs. The regress only applies when there is another individual of the same type, but necessity's essence is necessity itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the regress not apply to 'necessity'?
A) Because necessity is a considered thing, not real.
B) Because the essence of necessity is necessity itself, making it necessary by itself.
C) Because necessity is a relation that cannot have individuals.
D) Because the regress only applies to unity and determination.

Key Takeaway
Necessity is necessary by itself because its essence is necessity, thus no infinite chain of necessities is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the fact that only Allah is necessary by Himself. When you place your trust in any created thing, you are implicitly assuming it has a kind of self-sufficiency. How will you actively remind yourself today that every created thing is contingent and only Allah is self-necessary? What concrete step can you take to shift your reliance from creatures to the Creator?
?
The author argues that necessity is self-necessitating, so no regress is needed. Consider your own search for meaning and security: Are you looking for ultimate certainty in something that is not self-necessary? Will you commit to investigating where you truly place your foundation, and let the truth that only the Self-Necessary can ground your existence change how you live this week?

The Objection and Initial Answer
Objection and answer on describing non-existent quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against describing a non-existent quiddity with an existential quality and the initial answer that distinguishes between different conditions for subsistence.

Core Idea
Absence of posteriority does not prove possibility of priority.

In this passage, the commentator raises a subtle objection: just because a quality is not necessarily posterior to existence does not mean it can be prior, because it may be necessarily concurrent. The answer explores two possibilities: either existence is a condition for subsistence, making the quality posterior, or not, allowing separation. This introduces a key distinction in understanding rational judgments about qualities.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the objection
Read the objection carefully: from the absence of necessary posteriority, the permissibility of priority does not follow, because the quality might be necessarily concurrent with existence.
This is a logical point about modality.
2
Understand the logical move
Notice that the objection targets the inference from 'not necessary posterior' to 'possible prior'. The gap is filled by the possibility of necessary conjunction.
3
Analyze the answer's two branches
The answer divides based on whether existence or conjunction with existence is stipulated for subsistence. If yes, posteriority is necessary; if no, separation is possible, but then the objector counters with further conditions.
This shows how the discussion proceeds dialectically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لجاز اتصاف الماهية حال عدمها إلخ ) فيه بحث ظاهر إذ لا يلزم من عدم وجوب التأخر عن الوجود جواز التقدم عليه لجواز وجوب المقارنة معه فلا يلزم جواز اتصاف الماهية حال العدم بصفة وجودية. وقد يجاب بأنه إن اشترط في القيام الوجود أو المقارنة للوجود التي نسبة متوقفة على الوجود فالأمر ظاهر إذ حينئذ تكون الصفات مما يجب تأخرها عن وجود الموصوف ولا كلام فيها وإن لم يشترط لزم جواز اتصاف الماهية بها حال العدم نظراً إلى ذات الانفكاك تلك الصفة وإن فرض عدم بین الصفة والوجود في الواقع وفيه نظر لأن المجيب إن أوجب في الشرط تقدمه على المشروط منعنا الشرطية ولا يلزم من هذا جواز اتصاف الماهية بها حال العدم وإن لم يوجب سلمنا الشرطية بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك ولا يلزم منه وجوب تأخرها عن الموصوف بقى هنا بحث آخر وهو أن الشارح ذكر في حواشي التجريد من أن سبق الوجوب على الوجود ذاتي فليس الوجوب إلا في زمان الوجود وليس الامتناع إلا في زمان القدم فلا يلزم اجتماع المتنافيين في الوجوب والامتناع في زمان واحد ثم قال واندفع بهذا ما يقال من أن الوجوب أمر ثبوتي فكيف يتصف به الممكن حال عدمه وكلامه هاهنا ينافي ما ذكره في حواشي التجريد لأن المفهوم مما ذكره هناك أنه لو كان الوجوب صفة ثبوتية لجاز اتصاف الماهية به حال عدمها والمفهوم مما ذكروا هناك أن الوجوب صفة ثبوتية، لكن لا تتصف الماهية به إلا حال وجودها . وقد يجاب بأن معنى كلامه هاهنا أن الصفة التي لا يجب تأخرها عن موصوفها، لو كانت موجودة في الخارج لجاز عند العقل اتصاف الماهية حال عدمها في الخارج بصفة موجودة فيه، أي لم يحكم العقل ببديهته بامتناع قيام الصفة الموجودة بالموصوف المعدوم، مع أن العقل حاكم به
His statement: (For it would be permissible to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence etc.) In it there is an apparent discussion. For from the absence of the necessity of posteriority to existence, the permissibility of priority to it does not follow, because of the possibility of the necessity of conjunction with it. So the permissibility of describing the quiddity in the state of non-existence by an existential quality does not follow. And it may be answered that if existence or conjunction with existence, whose relation is dependent on existence, is stipulated for subsistence, then the matter is clear; for then the qualities would be among those that must be posterior to the existence of the qualified, and there is no discussion about them. And if it is not stipulated, then the permissibility of describing the quiddity by them in the state of non-existence follows, in consideration of the essence of separation of that quality, even if separation between the quality and existence is assumed in reality. And in it there is consideration, because if the answerer makes the condition that it precede the conditional, we deny the conditionality, and this does not entail the permissibility of describing the quiddity by it in the state of non-existence. And if he does not require that, we accept the conditionality in the sense of the impossibility of separation, and the necessity of its posteriority to the qualified does not follow. There remains here another discussion: that the commentator mentioned in the notes on al-Tajrid that the priority of necessity to existence is essential; so necessity is only in the time of existence, and impossibility is only in the time of pre-eternity; so the conjunction of the two opposites in necessity and impossibility in one time does not follow. Then he said: and with this is refuted what is said that necessity is a positive thing, so how can the possible be described by it in the state of non-existence? And his statement here contradicts what he mentioned in the notes on al-Tajrid, because what is understood from what he mentioned there is that if necessity were a positive quality, it would be permissible for the quiddity to be described by it in the state of non-existence; and what is understood from what is mentioned there is that necessity is a positive quality, but the quiddity is only described by it in the state of its existence. And it may be answered that the meaning of his statement here is that the quality which does not have to be posterior to its qualified, if it existed in the external world, it would be permissible according to reason to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence in the external world by an quality existing in it; i.e., reason does not judge by its intuition the impossibility of the subsistence of an existing quality in a non-existent qualified, while reason does judge it.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an extended objection-and-answer discussion. It begins with the commentator's remark on the phrase 'lajāza ittiṣāf al-māhiyya ḥāla ʿadamihā' (for it would be permissible to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence). The objection states that from the absence of the necessity of posteriority to existence, the permissibility of priority does not follow, because it could be that the quality is necessarily concurrent with existence. Hence, describing the quiddity while non-existent does not follow. Then an answer is presented: either the condition for subsistence is existence or conjunction with existence—if so, then these qualities must be posterior, no problem. If no such condition is stipulated, then it would be permissible for the quiddity to be described by the quality even when non-existent, considering the essence of separation of that quality. But the objector counters: if the answerer requires the condition to precede the conditioned, we deny the conditionality; if he does not, then we accept conditionality as impossibility of separation, but that does not entail posteriority. Then another discussion follows: the commentator (al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī?) in his notes on al-Tajrīd said that the priority of necessity to existence is essential; necessity only exists in the time of existence, impossibility only in the time of pre-eternity, so there is no contradiction. He used this to refute the claim that necessity is positive and yet describes the possible when non-existent. But here the commentator seems to contradict that. The author then harmonizes: what is meant here is that if a quality is not necessarily posterior to its qualified and existed externally, reason would not intuitively judge the impossibility of it subsisting in a non-existent qualified, even though reason does judge it by another proof.

Key Terms
وجوب التأخر
جواز التقدم
مقارنة
صفة وجودية
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a quality is not necessarily posterior to existence, it must be possible for it to be prior.
Correction: The quality could be necessarily concurrent with existence, which is a third possibility. Thus, non-necessity of posteriority does not automatically grant priority.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical point of the objection?
A) A quality must always be posterior to existence.
B) From absence of necessary posteriority, priority does not follow because the quality may be necessarily concurrent.
C) A quality can never describe a non-existent quiddity.
D) Existence is always a condition for subsistence.

Key Takeaway
The absence of necessary posteriority does not imply the possibility of priority; the quality may be necessarily concurrent, so describing a non-existent quiddity is not proven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume something is possible because you see no strict impossibility, do you ever forget that Allah might have decreed a necessary hidden condition that blocks that possibility? How does this logical lesson call you to humility before Allah's hidden wisdom?
?
Consider a time you relied on your own ability or goodness as if it existed independently of Allah's will. Is that not like describing the quiddity with a quality in non-existence? What would it take for you to truly see every quality of yours as contingent upon Allah's existence and grace?
?
If you find yourself saying 'I can do this because nothing prevents it,' how will you now check yourself with the lesson that absence of necessity does not prove possibility? Will you pause today to ask Allah to reveal any hidden necessities He has placed in your path?

The Commentator's Apparent Contradiction
Resolution of commentator's apparent inconsistency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the apparent contradiction in the commentator's views on necessity as a positive quality and how it is resolved through the role of rational judgment.

Core Idea
Necessity is positive yet only exists in the time of existence.

The passage then moves to a new issue: the commentator's own remarks in the notes on al-Tajrid seem to contradict his current argument. He previously argued that necessity is an essential priority to existence and only occurs in the time of existence, which refutes the claim that a positive quality cannot describe a non-existent. But now he appears to say the opposite. The resolution clarifies that his meaning is about rational judgment: reason does not intuitively reject the subsistence of an existent quality in a non-existent subject, though a proof may establish impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's statement from al-Tajrid notes
The commentator said that the priority of necessity to existence is essential, so necessity is only in the time of existence, impossibility only in pre-eternity, avoiding contradiction.
This was used to refute the objection that necessity is positive and cannot describe the non-existent possible.
2
Spot the apparent contradiction
Now the commentator's current statement seems to allow describing a quiddity with a positive quality in non-existence, which contradicts his earlier assertion that necessity is only in the time of existence.
The contradiction is between 'necessity is only in existence' and 'a non-existent can be described by a positive quality'.
3
Understand the resolution
The answer harmonizes: the commentator's current statement means that if a quality does not have to be posterior and existed externally, reason would not intuitively judge it impossible for it to subsist in a non-existent qualified, though reason does judge it impossible by another proof.
The key is that rational intuition (badīha) does not reject it, but a separate rational proof does.
4
Reflect on the role of reason
This shows that in kalam, immediate rational judgment (badīha) can be overridden by demonstrative proof. The commentator is not contradicting himself but distinguishing levels of rational certainty.

قوله : ( لجاز اتصاف الماهية حال عدمها إلخ ) فيه بحث ظاهر إذ لا يلزم من عدم وجوب التأخر عن الوجود جواز التقدم عليه لجواز وجوب المقارنة معه فلا يلزم جواز اتصاف الماهية حال العدم بصفة وجودية. وقد يجاب بأنه إن اشترط في القيام الوجود أو المقارنة للوجود التي نسبة متوقفة على الوجود فالأمر ظاهر إذ حينئذ تكون الصفات مما يجب تأخرها عن وجود الموصوف ولا كلام فيها وإن لم يشترط لزم جواز اتصاف الماهية بها حال العدم نظراً إلى ذات الانفكاك تلك الصفة وإن فرض عدم بین الصفة والوجود في الواقع وفيه نظر لأن المجيب إن أوجب في الشرط تقدمه على المشروط منعنا الشرطية ولا يلزم من هذا جواز اتصاف الماهية بها حال العدم وإن لم يوجب سلمنا الشرطية بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك ولا يلزم منه وجوب تأخرها عن الموصوف بقى هنا بحث آخر وهو أن الشارح ذكر في حواشي التجريد من أن سبق الوجوب على الوجود ذاتي فليس الوجوب إلا في زمان الوجود وليس الامتناع إلا في زمان القدم فلا يلزم اجتماع المتنافيين في الوجوب والامتناع في زمان واحد ثم قال واندفع بهذا ما يقال من أن الوجوب أمر ثبوتي فكيف يتصف به الممكن حال عدمه وكلامه هاهنا ينافي ما ذكره في حواشي التجريد لأن المفهوم مما ذكره هناك أنه لو كان الوجوب صفة ثبوتية لجاز اتصاف الماهية به حال عدمها والمفهوم مما ذكروا هناك أن الوجوب صفة ثبوتية، لكن لا تتصف الماهية به إلا حال وجودها . وقد يجاب بأن معنى كلامه هاهنا أن الصفة التي لا يجب تأخرها عن موصوفها، لو كانت موجودة في الخارج لجاز عند العقل اتصاف الماهية حال عدمها في الخارج بصفة موجودة فيه، أي لم يحكم العقل ببديهته بامتناع قيام الصفة الموجودة بالموصوف المعدوم، مع أن العقل حاكم به
His statement: (For it would be permissible to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence etc.) In it there is an apparent discussion. For from the absence of the necessity of posteriority to existence, the permissibility of priority to it does not follow, because of the possibility of the necessity of conjunction with it. So the permissibility of describing the quiddity in the state of non-existence by an existential quality does not follow. And it may be answered that if existence or conjunction with existence, whose relation is dependent on existence, is stipulated for subsistence, then the matter is clear; for then the qualities would be among those that must be posterior to the existence of the qualified, and there is no discussion about them. And if it is not stipulated, then the permissibility of describing the quiddity by them in the state of non-existence follows, in consideration of the essence of separation of that quality, even if separation between the quality and existence is assumed in reality. And in it there is consideration, because if the answerer makes the condition that it precede the conditional, we deny the conditionality, and this does not entail the permissibility of describing the quiddity by it in the state of non-existence. And if he does not require that, we accept the conditionality in the sense of the impossibility of separation, and the necessity of its posteriority to the qualified does not follow. There remains here another discussion: that the commentator mentioned in the notes on al-Tajrid that the priority of necessity to existence is essential; so necessity is only in the time of existence, and impossibility is only in the time of pre-eternity; so the conjunction of the two opposites in necessity and impossibility in one time does not follow. Then he said: and with this is refuted what is said that necessity is a positive thing, so how can the possible be described by it in the state of non-existence? And his statement here contradicts what he mentioned in the notes on al-Tajrid, because what is understood from what he mentioned there is that if necessity were a positive quality, it would be permissible for the quiddity to be described by it in the state of non-existence; and what is understood from what is mentioned there is that necessity is a positive quality, but the quiddity is only described by it in the state of its existence. And it may be answered that the meaning of his statement here is that the quality which does not have to be posterior to its qualified, if it existed in the external world, it would be permissible according to reason to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence in the external world by an quality existing in it; i.e., reason does not judge by its intuition the impossibility of the subsistence of an existing quality in a non-existent qualified, while reason does judge it.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an extended objection-and-answer discussion. It begins with the commentator's remark on the phrase 'lajāza ittiṣāf al-māhiyya ḥāla ʿadamihā' (for it would be permissible to describe the quiddity in the state of non-existence). The objection states that from the absence of the necessity of posteriority to existence, the permissibility of priority does not follow, because it could be that the quality is necessarily concurrent with existence. Hence, describing the quiddity while non-existent does not follow. Then an answer is presented: either the condition for subsistence is existence or conjunction with existence—if so, then these qualities must be posterior, no problem. If no such condition is stipulated, then it would be permissible for the quiddity to be described by the quality even when non-existent, considering the essence of separation of that quality. But the objector counters: if the answerer requires the condition to precede the conditioned, we deny the conditionality; if he does not, then we accept conditionality as impossibility of separation, but that does not entail posteriority. Then another discussion follows: the commentator (al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī?) in his notes on al-Tajrīd said that the priority of necessity to existence is essential; necessity only exists in the time of existence, impossibility only in the time of pre-eternity, so there is no contradiction. He used this to refute the claim that necessity is positive and yet describes the possible when non-existent. But here the commentator seems to contradict that. The author then harmonizes: what is meant here is that if a quality is not necessarily posterior to its qualified and existed externally, reason would not intuitively judge the impossibility of it subsisting in a non-existent qualified, even though reason does judge it by another proof.

سبق الوجوب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The commentator's statements in al-Tajrid and here are irreconcilably contradictory.
Correction: They are consistent: al-Tajrid's point is that necessity is only in existence to avoid contradiction; here the point is about the absence of immediate rational judgment (badīha) of impossibility, not about actual permissibility.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the resolution to the apparent contradiction?
A) The commentator made a mistake in al-Tajrid.
B) Reason does not immediately judge the impossibility of an existing quality subsisting in a non-existent qualified, though a proof may establish it.
C) Necessity is not a positive quality.
D) The quiddity can never be described by a quality in non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's statements are consistent: immediate rational intuition does not reject the subsistence of an existent quality in a non-existent subject, but a demonstrative proof does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an apparent contradiction in Islamic teachings, do you impatiently dismiss it based on your first impression, or do you patiently seek the deeper resolution? This lesson shows that immediate intuition can mislead. Will you commit today to suspending judgment until you have thoroughly investigated any such conflict in your own understanding?
?
The commentator's two statements seemed to clash until the distinction between immediate intuition and demonstrative proof was made. Is there a belief you hold about your faith that you have only accepted based on a 'gut feeling' without seeking solid evidence? Where can you start today to find the demonstration that either confirms or corrects that feeling?
?
This passage highlights the precision required in kalam reasoning. How often do you reflect on the rational foundations of your aqeedah? Can you name one specific area where your conviction is based on tradition or emotion rather than rational proof, and what step will you take this week to strengthen it?

Distinguishing Kalam Modal Notions from Logical Modalities
Al-Jurjani distinguishes kalam modal notions from logical modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the modal notions of wajib, mumkin, and mumtani' in kalam are more specific than their logical counterparts, and how they apply specifically to existence itself.

Core Idea
Kalam's necessity, possibility, impossibility are about existence itself, not about any predicate.

Al-Jurjani begins by drawing a crucial line: the three modal categories that form the foundation of kalam are not the same as the logical modalities you learned about in logic. While logic deals with necessity, possibility, and impossibility of any predicate (like 'Zayd is black'), kalam is concerned exclusively with the necessity, possibility, or impossibility of existence. This distinction prevents confusion about what it means for Allah to be 'wajib al-wujud' versus something like 'evenness being necessary for four'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the key terms jihaat and mawaad.
2
Identify the two domains
The passage contrasts two uses of wajib/mumkin/mumtani: one in logic (as modalities/matters of propositions) and one in kalam (as applied to existence). Focus on how the author explains why kalam usage is more specific.
3
Understand the example of 'four is even'
The author uses the example of evenness being necessary for four to show that this is a logical necessity of predication, not an ontological necessity of existence. Work through this example to grasp the distinction.
This exemplifies the difference between essential necessity (wujub dhati) and predicative necessity (wujub al-haml).
4
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: Why does al-Jurjani insist on this distinction? How does it protect a student of kalam from mistakenly treating accidental properties as necessary existents? The commentary will help.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع التي نحن فيها غير الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع التي هي جهات القضايا في التعقل أو الذكر وموادها بحسب نفس الأمر، وذلك لأن المبحوث عنها هاهنا وجوب الوجود وامتناع الوجود، وإمكان الوجود والعدم، فهي جهات ومواد في قضايا مخصوصة محمولاتها وجود الشيء في نفسه فتكون أخص من جهات القضايا وموادها فإن المحمول في القضية قد يكون وجود الشيء في نفسه، وقد يكون مفهوماً آخر، وحينئذ إما أن يعتبر وجود ذلك المفهوم للموضوع حقيقة كالسواد في قولنا : زيد أسود وإما أن يعتبر مجرد اتصاف الموضوع بذلك المفهوم الاعتباري الذي لا وجود له في الخارج، كالعمى في قولنا : زيد أعمى . والوجوب والإمكان والامتناع التي هي جهات القضايا وموادها جارية في الكل، فيقال : زيد يجب أن يكون أسود أو أعمى أو يمتنع أو يمكن كما يقال : زيد يجب وجوده أو يمتنع أو يمكن وهذا الأخير هو الذي نحن بصدده إذ مرادنا بالواجب هاهنا هو الواجب الوجود لا الواجب الحيوانية أو السوادية أو غيرهما، وكذا الحال في الممتنع والممكن وإلا أي وإن لم تكن هذه غير جهات القضايا وموادها بل كانت عينها لكانت لوازم الماهيات واجبة لذواتها أي كانت تلك اللوازم من قبيل الواجب الذي نحن نبحث عنه، وليست كذلك. فإذا قلنا مثلاً: الزوجية واجبة للأربعة فنعني به وجوب الحمل أي حمل الزوجية على الأربعة وامتناع الانفكاك أي انفكاك الأربعة عن صفة الزوجية. وهذا أي وجوب الحمل الذي بين الأربعة والزوجية غير الوجوب الذاتي الذي بين الشيء ووجوده، ألا ترى أن الأربعة واجبة الزوجية لا واجبة الوجود وأن الزوجية واجبة الحمل والصدق على الأربعة لا واجبة الوجود في نفسها، وتحقيقه ما صورناه لك فلا تغفل عنه.
Know that these [notions of] wajib, mumkin, and mumtani' which we are discussing are different from the wajib, mumkin, and mumtani' that are the modalities (jihaat) of propositions in intellection or utterance and their matters (mawaad) according to the actual state of affairs. That is because what is investigated here is the necessity of existence, the impossibility of existence, and the possibility of existence and non-existence. So they are modalities and matters in specific propositions whose predicates are the existence of the thing in itself. Thus they are more specific than the modalities of propositions and their matters, because the predicate in a proposition may be the existence of the thing in itself, or it may be another concept. Then it is either considered that the existence of that concept inheres in the subject truly, like blackness in our saying 'Zayd is black', or it is considered merely the description of the subject by that conceptual attribute which has no existence in the external, like blindness in our saying 'Zayd is blind'. And the wajib, mumkin, and mumtani' that are the modalities and matters of propositions apply to all, so it is said: 'Zayd must be black or blind' or 'is impossible' or 'possible', just as it is said: 'Zayd's existence is necessary' or 'impossible' or 'possible'. And this latter is what we are dealing with, because our intended meaning of 'wajib' here is the necessarily existent, not the necessary of animality or blackness or others. And similarly for the mumtani' and mumkin. Otherwise (i.e., if these were not distinct from the modalities of propositions and their matters but were identical to them), then the necessary concomitants of essences would be necessary in themselves, i.e., those concomitants would be of the category of the wajib that we are investigating, and they are not so. For example, when we say 'evenness is necessary for four', we mean by it the necessity of predication (i.e., predicating evenness of four) and the impossibility of separation (i.e., four being separated from the attribute of evenness). And this (i.e., the necessity of predication between four and evenness) is other than the essential necessity which is between a thing and its existence. Do you not see that four is necessarily even, not necessarily existent, and that evenness is necessarily predicated and true of four, not necessarily existent in itself. Its verification is as we have depicted it for you, so do not neglect it.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the three modal notions (wajib, mumkin, mumtani') as they are used in kalam (theology) from their use in logic as modalities and matters of propositions. The author begins by stating that the three notions under discussion are different from the logical ones. The reason is that in kalam, we are concerned specifically with necessity, impossibility, and possibility of existence itself (wujub al-wujud, imtina' al-wujud, imkan al-wujud wa al-'adam). Thus they are modalities in propositions where the predicate is 'existence of the thing in itself', making them more specific than logical modalities which apply to any predicate. The author illustrates with examples: in logic, we say 'Zayd must be black' (necessity of a predicate other than existence), whereas in kalam, we say 'Zayd's existence is necessary'. Our intended meaning of 'wajib' here is the necessarily existent being, not the necessity of a property like animality or blackness. The passage then argues that if these kalam notions were identical to logical modalities, then necessary concomitants of essences (like evenness for four) would be necessary in themselves—which they are not. Instead, when we say 'evenness is necessary for four', we mean the necessity of predication (wujub al-haml) and the impossibility of separation, not the essential necessity (wujub dhati) that obtains between a thing and its existence. The key distinction is that essential necessity concerns existence itself, whereas logical necessity concerns the relation between subject and predicate. The passage ends by urging the reader not to neglect this verification.

إمكان الوجود
امتناع الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think that when we say 'evenness is necessary for four', we are using the same kind of necessity as when we say 'Allah's existence is necessary'.
Correction: As the passage explains, the necessity of evenness for four is a necessity of predication (wujub al-haml) and is not about existence itself. In kalam, we only use these modal categories for existence, not for attributes or essences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between the modal notions (wajib, mumkin, mumtani') as used in kalam and as used in logic?
A) Kalam deals with these notions only in propositions, while logic deals with them in essences.
B) In kalam, they are predicated specifically of existence itself, while in logic they can apply to any predicate.
C) In kalam, they are considered non-existential, while in logic they are existential.
D) There is no difference; they are the same notions used in different fields.

Key Takeaway
The modal categories wajib, mumkin, and mumtani in kalam are exclusively about existence itself, distinguishing them from the broader logical modalities that apply to any predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make claims like 'I must be honest' or 'I must pray', are you treating honesty and prayer as necessarily existent in themselves (wajib al-wujud) or as necessarily predicated of a believer (wajib al-haml)? How does confusing these two necessities affect your actual commitment to these acts?
?
Think of something you consider 'necessary' in your life—a habit, a relationship, a possession. Is it necessary in existence (like your own life) or only necessary as a description (like 'this car must be fast')? How would distinguishing these two types of necessity change the way you hold to that thing?
?
The passage warns against confusing the necessity of predication with essential necessity. In your spiritual life, have you ever mistaken a conceptual certainty (e.g., 'God is merciful') for a existentially lived reality? What would it take for you to move from affirming that 'God is merciful' as a true predicate to experiencing His mercy as necessary for your very existence?

First Argument: Wajib Must Be Existential and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the first argument that wajib is existential and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disputant's argument that wajib cannot be non-existential because the Necessary is necessary in Himself, and see how it is refuted.

Core Idea
Necessity is real, not mind-dependent.

Al-Jurjani engages with a disputant who argues that wajib (necessity) must be an existential reality because if it were non-existential, it would depend on the mind's consideration. But the Necessary Being is necessary in Himself, independent of any mind. This passage presents that argument and its refutation, showing that the objection does not affect the kalām position that modal judgments are mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The argument uses an if-then premise: if wajib were non-existential, then it would depend on the mind. Identify this conditional.
3
Understand why the consequent is false
The conclusion that the Necessary would be necessary only when considered is false because the Necessary is necessary in Himself, regardless of any mind.
4
Note the refutation phrase
The phrase 'lam yaqdah' (it does not harm) shows the objection is refuted. Understand its significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد زعم بعض المجادلين أنها أي هذه الأمور الثلاثة سوى الامتناع إذ لم يدع أحد كونه وجودياً أمور وجودية لوجوه ثلاثة جارية في كل واحد من الوجوب والإمكان الأول: الوجوب لو كان أمراً عدمياً لم يتحقق إلا باعتبار العقل له إذ لا تحقق للعدميات في أنفسها إنما تحققها باعتبار العقل لها فيلزم أن لا يكون الواجب واجباً إلا إذا اعتبر العقل وجوبه والتالي باطل ؛ فإن الواجب واجب في نفسه مع قطع النظر عن غيره ، سواء وجد فرض من عقل أم لا يوجد فرض أصلا، بل ولو فرض عدم العقول كلها. وحينئذ لا يتصور أن يوجد منها فرض الوجوب قطعاً لم يقدح
And some disputants have claimed that these (i.e., these three notions except for impossibility, since no one claimed it is existential) are existential realities, based on three arguments that apply to each of wajib and mumkin. The first: If wajib were a non-existential matter, it would not be realized except by reason's consideration, since non-existentials have no realization in themselves; their realization is only by reason's consideration. Then it would follow that the Necessary would not be necessary unless reason considers its necessity. But the consequent is false, because the Necessary is necessary in Himself, disregarding anything else, whether a supposition from a reason exists or not, and even if it is supposed that all minds are nonexistent. Then it is impossible that any supposition of necessity would exist from them, certainly that does not harm.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding the ontological status of the modal notions, specifically the claim that wajib (necessity) is an existential reality (wujudiyya) rather than a non-existential (i'tibari) concept. The disputant argues that if wajib were non-existential, it would only be realized through the consideration of a mind, since non-existentials (al-'adamiyyat) have no reality in themselves. Consequently, the Necessary Being would only be necessary when a mind considers His necessity. This consequence is false, because the Necessary is necessary in Himself (wajib fi nafsihi), regardless of any mind's supposition—even if all minds are supposed nonexistent. Since the consequent is false, the antecedent must also be false; thus wajib is not non-existential but existential. The refutation is presented as part of the matn: the falsity of the consequent is asserted, and the argument that the objection does not harm the kalam position is indicated by 'lam yaqdah' (it does not harm). The commentary (which continues after this excerpt) explains the reasoning further, but the core objection and refutation are complete in these lines.

اعتبار العقل
العدميات
الواجب في نفسه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that necessity is merely a mental concept with no reality in the divine essence.
Correction: The refutation shows that necessity is real and not dependent on the mind; the Necessary is necessary in Himself.

Checkpoint
What is the disputant's first argument for claiming that wajib is existential?
A) If wajib were non-existential, it would depend on the mind, but the Necessary is necessary in Himself, so wajib must be existential.
B) The Necessary Being cannot be necessary if necessity is only a mental concept.
C) The existence of the Necessary requires a cause, so necessity must be existential.
D) All three modal notions are recognized by the mind as real.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the existential nature of wajib is refuted by affirming that the Necessary is necessary in Himself, independent of any mind's consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Allah is necessary in Himself, independent of your thoughts, or does your certainty about Him fluctuate with your intellectual understanding? What would it take for your conviction to be as firm as this passage demands—rooted in His reality, not your mind?
?
When you face doubts about Allah’s existence or attributes, are you tempted to think that if you cannot fully grasp a proof, then His necessity might be uncertain? How does this passage’s insistence that He is necessary in Himself, even if no mind exists, challenge that tendency?
?
If all minds were to cease, Allah would still be necessary—yet you often act as if your faith depends on feeling intellectually convinced. What would change in your worship today if you truly internalized that your mental state does not affect His reality at all?

The Disputant's First Proof for Wujub and Imkan Being Existential
Disputant's claim and first proof for wujub and imkan being existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disputant's argument that if wujub were non-existential, God's necessity would depend on an intellect, which is false, so wujub must be existential.

Core Idea
God's necessity is independent of any mind; therefore necessity cannot be a mere mental consideration.

The discussion concerns whether the rational judgments of wujub (necessity) and imkan (possibility) are existential realities or mere mental constructs. A disputant argues they must be existential, and provides his first proof: if wujub were non-existential, it would only exist in the mind, making God's necessity dependent on a thinker—an absurd conclusion. This lesson unpacks that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقد زعم بعض المجادلين' to 'لم يقدح' carefully, along with the translation. Identify the claim, the conditional (if…then), and the reason given for the consequent being false.
The passage uses a conditional structure: if non-existential → then dependent on intellect → false consequent.
2
Understand the terms 'adami and wujudi
Note that 'adami means non-existential (a mental consideration only) and wujudi means existential (has reality outside the mind). The disputant aims to prove that wujub and imkan are wujudi.
The disputant excludes impossibility (imtina') because no one claims it is existential.
3
Analyze the proof's logic
Break down the proof: Premise 1: Non-existentials are realized only by intellectual consideration. Premise 2: If wujub were non-existential, then God would be necessary only when an intellect considers His necessity. Premise 3: But God is necessary in Himself regardless of any intellect. Conclusion: Therefore, wujub is not non-existential; it is existential.
The same reasoning applies to imkan.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The disputant concludes that wujub and imkan are existential. Consider how this contrasts with the author's position that these are 'considerational matters' with no external existence.
This sets up the author's refutation in later passages.

The Third Observatory - The Second Purpose: Considerational matters that have no existence in the external world. 125. ... between four and evenness (other than essential necessity) which is between a thing and its existence. Do you not see that four is necessarily even, not necessarily existent, and that evenness is necessarily predicated and true of four, not necessarily existent in itself. Its verification is what we have depicted for you, so do not neglect it. And some disputants have claimed (that these three matters, except impossibility, since no one has claimed it to be existential, are existential matters, due to three proofs that apply to each of necessity and possibility. The first: if necessity were a non-existential matter, it would not be realized except by the consideration of the intellect, since non-existentials have no realization in themselves; their realization is only by the intellect's consideration of them. Then it would follow that the Necessary would not be necessary unless the intellect considered His necessity. But the consequent is false, for the Necessary is necessary in Himself, regardless of anything else, whether a supposition of an intellect exists or no supposition exists at all, even if the non-existence of all intellects were supposed. And then it is impossible that a supposition of necessity exists from them at all; it does not detract. His statement: ('since non-existentials have no realization') means attributes that are non-existent in themselves, for if they were realized in themselves they would be existent accidents in the external world, not non-existent attributes. His statement: ('then it would follow, etc.') because what has no realization except by the consideration of the intellect does not occur as an attribute of something except by its consideration. His statement: ('regardless of anything else') i.e., other than what? It is an interpretation of His statement 'in Himself'. He gave a general interpretation and did not interpret it as 'regardless of the consideration of the intellect' in order to validate the generalization derived from His statement: 'whether a supposition of an intellect exists or not.' His statement: ('even if the non-existence of intellects were supposed') i.e., in terms of their being intellects, meaning the supposition of the negation of the attribute of intellection from all perceptive faculties, even the Necessary also. For supposing Him devoid of knowledge is possible, even if the supposed thing is impossible. His statement: ('it is impossible, etc.') because the supposition of necessity is dependent on considering intellection with them. His statement: ('does not detract, etc.') because His necessity is prior to the perception of all high principles, even His own knowledge also. And by what we have set forth, the confusion of the contemplators is repelled: if by 'intellects' is meant the lower faculties, it does not benefit, because it is possible that His description with necessity is in the high faculties; and if by them is meant the general [including] lower and high... His statement: ('but even if the non-existence of intellects were supposed') the context of his speech here indicates that the possible, for example, is described with possibility on the supposition of the negation of all perceptive faculties. Then their statement is problematic: 'the establishment of a thing for a thing is dependent on the establishment of that for which it is established,' for there is no establishment of the described here in the external world, because the non-existent is described with possibility during its non-existence, and not in the mind because the supposition is the non-existence of any mind. The truth is that the context of the speech here is based on the claim of some disputants, and I have previously alerted to the repulsion of the problem, so let it be remembered. For if you say: if into the supposition of the non-existence of intellects there was included the supposition of the non-existence of high principles, even the non-existence of the Necessary — exalted be He above that greatly — then the Necessary would not be described with necessity at all; and if it were not included, this speech would not be directed, because it does not follow from His being non-existential that He is only realized by the consideration of our intellect, due to the possibility of His realization by the consideration of the supposition of high principles. I say: it is included in this individual. 126. The Third Observatory - The Second Purpose: Considerational matters that have no existence in the external world. That [does not detract] from the necessity of the Necessary, and does not remove the Necessary from being necessary. And the same holds for possibility, so each of them is existential.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the claim of a disputant who argues that necessity (wujub) and possibility (imkan) are existential (wujudiyya) rather than non-existential ('adamiyya). The passage begins by setting the context: the discussion concerns 'considerational matters' (umur i'tibariyya) that have no external existence. The disputant excludes impossibility (imtina') from the discussion, as no one claims it is existential. He then provides three proofs (wujuh) that apply to both wujub and imkan. Only the first proof is elaborated here. The first proof argues that if wujub were non-existential ('adami), it would have no realization except through the consideration of an intellect. Consequently, the Necessary (al-Wajib, i.e., God) would only be necessary when an intellect considers His necessity. This conclusion is absurd, because God is necessary in Himself, independent of any external consideration, even if no intellect exists. Therefore, wujub cannot be non-existential; it must be existential. The commentary that follows clarifies key points: 'non-existentials' are attributes that have no realization in themselves (otherwise they would be accidents), and the proof's logic is that what is only realized by the intellect cannot be a real attribute of an external entity. The commentary also addresses the objection about 'high principles' (al-mabadi' al-aliya) such as God's own knowledge, explaining that God's necessity precedes even His own knowledge. It also tackles a potential problem: if all intellects are removed, how can a possible thing be described with possibility? The answer is that the context of the speech is based on the disputant's assumption, and the problem was already resolved earlier. The passage concludes by stating that the same reasoning applies to possibility (imkan), so both wujub and imkan are existential.
